Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
6 min
Diyat for Jaa'ifah Wound: Non-Penetrating and Penetrating
States the diyat for jaa'ifah with proof from hadith and Abu Bakr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the diyat for a jaa'ifah wound (non-penetrating and penetrating) and explain the proof from hadith and the reasoning of Abu Bakr.

Core Idea
A non-penetrating jaa'ifah incurs one-third diyat; a penetrating one incurs two-thirds—each penetration is treated as a separate jaa'ifah.

This lesson examines the diyat (monetary compensation) for a specific type of wound called jaa'ifah, which penetrates the body cavity. The Hanafi school, relying on hadith and the practice of Abu Bakr, distinguishes between wounds that do not go through the body and those that do. Understanding this ruling clarifies how Islamic law quantifies compensation for internal injuries.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of jaa'ifah
Distinguish between a non-penetrating jaa'ifah (wound reaches cavity but does not exit) and a penetrating jaa'ifah (wound goes through to the other side). The ruling differs for each.
2
Recall the diyat amounts
Non-penetrating: one-third of the full diyat. Penetrating: two-thirds of the full diyat. Memorize these amounts as they are fixed in the Hanafi school.
3
Examine the dalil
The primary proof is the hadith from the book of Amr ibn Hazm where the Prophet (peace be upon him) specifies one-third for jaa'ifah. The penetrating case is supported by the ruling of Abu Bakr and qiyas.
4
Understand the qiyas
A penetrating wound is treated as two separate jaa'ifahs: one entering from the front and one exiting from the back. Each incurs one-third diyat, total two-thirds.
This is a clear application of qiyas jali (clear analogy).
5
Note the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds this as the mufta bihi position, based on authentic transmission and rational analogy.

اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد - ۱۵ -- آمہ میں تہائی دیت واجب ہوگی۔ - جائفہ میں بھی تہائی دیت واجب ہوگی۔ - سوال جائفہ کیا چیز ہے؟ ۱۵۵ كتاب الديات جواب جاکفہ وہ زخم ہے جو جوف تک پہنچ جائے، خواہ سینہ میں ہو یا پیٹ میں یا کمر میں وغیرہ وغیرہ۔ سوال یہاں تو شجاج کا ذکر چل رہا ہے تو جا ئفہ کا ذکر کیوں آ گیا ؟ جواب صاحب نہایہ نے یہ کہا ہے کہ یہ ذکر یہاں اتفاق آ گیا ہے ورنہ شجاج فقط اس زخم کو کہتے ہیں جو سر اور چہرے میں ہوتا ہے۔ پھر فرمایا کہ جا ئفہ میں تہائی دیت جب واجب ہوگی ، جب کہ وہ زخم پار نہ ہوا ہو۔ ورنہ اگر دوسری جانب پار ہو گیا تو پھر دبیت سے دو ثلث واجب ہوں گے۔ مسئلہ مذکورہ کی دلیل لماروى في كتاب عمرو بن حزم ان النبي عليه السلام قال وفى الموضحة خمس من الابل وفي الهاشمة عشر وفى المنقلة خمسة عشر وفى الامة ويروى المامومة ثلث الدية وقال عليه السلام في الجائفة ثلث الدية وعن ابى بكر انه حكم في جائفة نفذت الى الجانب الاخر بثلثى الدية ولا نها اذا نفذت نزلت منزلة جائفتين احدهما من جانب البطن والاخرى من جانب الظهر وفى كل جائفة ثلث الدية فلهذا وجب في النافذة ثلثا الدية ترجمہ بوجہ اس کے کہ عمرو بن حزم کی کتاب میں مروی ہے کہ نبی کریم صلى الله عليه وسلم نے فرمایا اور موضحہ میں پانچ اونٹ ہیں اور ہاشمہ میں دس اونٹ ہیں اور منتقلہ میں پندرہ اور آمہ میں، اور مروی ہے مامومہ میں تہائی دیت ہے اور نبی علیہ السلام نے فرمایا کہ جائفہ میں تہائی دیت ہے اور ابوبکڑ سے مروی ہے کہ انہوں نے اس جائفہ میں جو دوسری جانب پار ہو جائے دیت کے دو ثلث کا حکم فرمایا، اور اس لئے کہ جائفہ جب کہ پار ہو جائے تو اس کو دو جا ئفہ کے درجہ میں اتار لیا جائے گا ایک اندر کی جانب سے اور دوسرا باہر کی جانب سے اور ہر جا ئفہ میں تہائی دیت ہے اسی وجہ سے نافذ ( جو پار ہو جائے ) میں دو ثلث دیت ہو گی ۔ تشریح امام قدوری نے جو مسائل بیان فرمائے تھے ان تمام مسائل کے تین اجزاء ہیں تو دلیل کے بھی تعین جز ہیں۔ - موضحہ میں پانچ اونٹ اور ہاشمہ میں دس اور منقلہ میں پندرہ اور آمہ میں تہائی دیت اس کی دلیل میں عمرو بن حزم کی کتاب کی پہلی حدیث بیان فرمائی۔ جائفہ میں ثلث دیت ہے اس کے ثبوت میں دوسری حدیث پیش فرمائی۔ - اگر جا کفہ ایک جانب پار ہو جائے تو اس کے ثبوت میں حضرت صدیق اکبر کا فیصلہ پیش فرمایا ہے۔ دوسری دلیل عقلی پیش فرمائی کہ جب جا کفہ پار ہو گیا تو اب وہ ایک جائفہ نہیں رہا بلکہ دو ہوگئے اور ہر جائفہ میں تہائی دیت واجب ہوتی ہے۔ لہذا دو میں دو ثلث واجب ہوگی۔
Ashraf al-Hidaya Urdu commentary of Hidaya, volume 15: In aamah, one-third diyat is obligatory. In jaa'ifah also, one-third diyat is obligatory. Question: What is jaa'ifah? Answer: Jaa'ifah is a wound that reaches the cavity (jauf), whether in the chest, stomach, back, etc. Question: Here the discussion is about shijaj, so why mention jaa'ifah? Answer: The author of al-Nihayah said that this mention came incidentally, otherwise shijaj refers only to wounds on the head and face. Then he said: In jaa'ifah, one-third diyat is obligatory when the wound has not penetrated through. But if it penetrates to the other side, then two-thirds of the diyat are obligatory. Proof of the mentioned ruling: Based on what is narrated in the book of Amr ibn Hazm that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'In al-mudihah, five camels; in al-hashimah, ten; in al-munaqqilah, fifteen; in al-ammah (and it is also narrated as al-ma'mumah), one-third of the diyat.' And the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'In al-jaa'ifah, one-third of the diyat.' And it is narrated from Abu Bakr that he ruled in a jaa'ifah that penetrated to the other side with two-thirds of the diyat, because when it penetrates, it is treated as two jaa'ifahs: one from the side of the stomach and the other from the side of the back, and in each jaa'ifah, one-third of the diyat. Thus, in the penetrating wound, two-thirds diyat is obligatory. Translation: Because it is narrated in the book of Amr ibn Hazm that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'In al-mudihah, five camels; in al-hashimah, ten; in al-munaqqilah, fifteen; and in al-ammah (and it is narrated as al-ma'mumah), one-third diyat.' And the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'In jaa'ifah, one-third diyat.' And it is narrated from Abu Bakr that he ruled in a jaa'ifah that penetrates to the other side with two-thirds diyat, and because a jaa'ifah when it penetrates is reduced to the level of two jaa'ifahs: one from inside and the other from outside. And in every jaa'ifah, one-third diyat is due. Hence, in a penetrating wound, two-thirds diyat is due. Explanation: Imam al-Quduri mentioned these rulings in three parts. The proof also has three parts: The first hadith from the book of Amr ibn Hazm is for mudihah, hashimah, munaqqilah, and aamah with one-third. The second hadith is for jaa'ifah with one-third. If the jaa'ifah penetrates one side, the proof is the decision of Abu Bakr al-Siddiq. The second rational proof: when the jaa'ifah penetrates, it is no longer one jaa'ifah but two, and each requires one-third diyat, so two-thirds are obligatory.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage deals with the ruling on jaa'ifah wounds. Jaa'ifah is a wound that penetrates the body cavity (chest, stomach, back, etc.). The author first states that both aamah (head wound reaching the brain) and jaa'ifah incur one-third diyat if not penetrating. Then he clarifies that if the jaa'ifah penetrates through the body, it becomes two-thirds diyat. This is supported by a hadith from the book of Amr ibn Hazm and the practice of Abu Bakr. The rational explanation is that a penetrating wound is considered two separate jaa'ifahs, each entailing one-third diyat. The tashreeh (commentary) explains that the proof is divided into three parts corresponding to three sets of rulings: the first hadith covers the first five shijaj (mudihah, hashimah, munaqqilah, aamah), the second hadith covers jaa'ifah, and the ruling from Abu Bakr covers the penetrating case. The overall lesson is that diyat for head and body wounds is quantified according to severity and penetration.

Key Terms
جائفہ (jaa'ifah): A wound that reaches the body cavity (jauf), e.g., chest, stomach, back.
دية (diyah): Monetary compensation for bodily harm or homicide, assessed in camels or equivalent value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the diyat for a jaa'ifah wound that penetrates through both sides of the body?
A) One-third of the full diyat
B) Half of the full diyat
C) Two-thirds of the full diyat
D) The full diyat

Key Takeaway
A penetrating jaa'ifah wound is treated as two separate jaa'ifahs, each incurring one-third diyat, totaling two-thirds.

The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Mutalahimah and Badi'ah
Defines two head wound types and notes a verbal dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference in definition between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding mutalahimah and badi'ah, and identify that the dispute is purely terminological without affecting the legal ruling.

Core Idea
A difference in expression ('ibarah) does not change the legal ruling; both wounds require hukumat al-'adl.

In the Hanafi school, precise definitions of wound types are important for diyat. However, even among the founding imams, there can be verbal disagreements that do not affect the legal outcome. This lesson examines a difference between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on the order and definition of two head wounds: mutalahimah and badi'ah. The passage clarifies that this is merely an 'ikhtilaf 'ibarah (difference in expression) with no impact on the ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad
Identify what each imam says about the order and definition of mutalahimah and badi'ah. Abu Yusuf places badi'ah fourth and mutalahimah fifth; Muhammad reverses the order and defines mutalahimah as a wound where blood gathers and blackens.
Their definitions differ: Abu Yusuf focuses on flesh-cutting, Muhammad on blood accumulation.
2
Analyze the linguistic basis
Consider the Arabic roots: Abu Yusuf's definition derives from lahm (flesh), while Muhammad's derives from iltiham (gathering). This explains the different descriptions.
Both roots are valid for the word mutalahimah.
3
Read the author's reconciliation
The passage states 'this is a difference of expression that does not return to meaning or judgment.' Recognize that the author dismisses the dispute as merely verbal.
The author does not prefer one definition over the other.
4
Determine the legal effect
Both wounds, regardless of definition, are treated the same under Hanafi law: they are shijaj that require hukumat al-'adl (government assessment) rather than a fixed arsh.
The practical ruling (hukm) is identical.
5
Apply the broader principle
Learn that not every difference in wording among scholars implies a difference in legal outcome. This principle applies across fiqh.
This is known as ikhtilaf 'ibarah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن محمد انه جعل المتلاحمة قبل الباضعة وقال هى التى يتلاحم فيها الدم ويسود وما ذكرناه بد أمروى عن أبي يوسف وهذا اختلاف عبارة لا يعود الى معنى وحكم
And from Muhammad that he placed mutalahimah before badi'ah and said: It is that in which blood gathers and becomes black. And what we mentioned, [؟] from Abu Yusuf. And this is a difference of expression that does not return to meaning or judgment.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage presents a difference between two major Hanafi imams, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, regarding the order and definition of two types of head wounds: mutalahimah and badi'ah. According to Abu Yusuf (as mentioned earlier in the text), badi'ah is fourth and mutalahimah is fifth. However, Muhammad reverses this order and defines mutalahimah as a wound where blood gathers and the skin becomes black. The author notes that this is merely a difference in wording and does not affect the legal ruling. In terms of hukm, both wounds have the same arsh (fixed compensation) under hukumat al-'adl. The difference is only in the linguistic derivation: Abu Yusuf derives mutalahimah from lahm (flesh), while Muhammad derives it from iltiham (gathering).

Key Terms
متلاحمة (mutalahimah): A head wound that reaches the flesh; according to Abu Yusuf, it is after badi'ah; according to Muhammad, it is before badi'ah and defined as a wound where blood gathers and becomes black.
باضعة (badi'ah): A head wound that splits the skin and makes the flesh visible but does not reach the bone.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the difference between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding mutalahimah?
A) They disagree on the amount of compensation (arsh).
B) They differ on the definition and order but agree on the ruling.
C) Muhammad considers it a fatal wound.
D) Abu Yusuf considers it a wound of the bone.

Key Takeaway
A difference in expression ('ibarah) between Hanafi imams on the definition of a wound does not affect the legal ruling; both definitions lead to hukumat al-'adl.

Definition of Damighah and Its Exclusion from Shijaj
Defines the eleventh head wound and explains its omission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the damighah, explain why it is not enumerated among the shijaj, and state that its ruling falls under homicide rather than fixed arsh.

Core Idea
Damighah is a fatal head wound; it is omitted from shijaj because it kills, not merely wounds.

The eleventh head wound, ad-damighah, penetrates to the brain and almost always results in death. Unlike the ten shijaj which have fixed amounts of blood money (arsh) for non-fatal injuries, the damighah is treated as a homicide. This lesson explains the definition and the legal reasoning for its exclusion from the detailed list of head wounds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wound
Name the wound: damighah. Recognize that it is the eleventh type after the ten enumerated shijaj.
2
Recognize the anatomical depth
The wound reaches the brain (tasal ila al-dimagh). This is the defining characteristic.
The skull is breached.
3
Understand the fatal implication
Such a wound generally causes death (taqa' qatlan fi al-ghalib). It is not survivable in most cases.
4
Connect to legal consequence
Because it is fatal, it is not a 'limited injury' (jinaayah muqtasirah) with a separate ruling. Instead, it falls under the laws of homicide (jinayat al-nafs): qisas if intentional, diyat if accidental.
5
Appreciate the wisdom of omission
Imam Muhammad omitted it from the shijaj list because the shijaj are specifically for non-fatal wounds with fixed arsh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد هذا شجة اخرى تسمى الدامغة وهى التى تصل الى الدماغ وانما لم يذكرها لانها تقع قتلا في الغالب لا جناية مقتصرة مفردة بحكم على حدة
And after this there is another wound called ad-damighah, and it is that which reaches the brain. He (Muhammad) did not mention it because it generally results in death, not a limited injury with a separate ruling.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage discusses the damighah, the eleventh type of head wound after amm (the tenth). It penetrates the skull to reach the brain. The author explains why Imam Muhammad did not include it in his enumeration of shijaj: because such a wound almost always causes death. Therefore, it is not treated as a separate shajjah with its own fixed arsh, but rather as a fatal injury subject to the laws of qisas (retaliation) if intentional, or diyat (blood money) if accidental. The focus of the shijaj is on non-fatal wounds where the victim survives, so the damighah is excluded from that category.

Key Terms
دامغة (damighah): A head wound that reaches the brain, almost always fatal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it never occurs.
B) Because it is always accidental.
C) Because it almost always results in death, so it falls under the ruling of homicide.
D) Because it has no legal consequence.

Key Takeaway
Ad-damighah is excluded from shijaj because it is typically fatal, so its ruling is that of killing, not a fixed arsh wound.

Shijaj vs Jirahah: The Scope of Fixed Arsh
Establishes linguistic and legal distinction between shijaj and jirahah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish shijaj from jirahah based on legal and linguistic criteria, and state the compensation rule for each.

Core Idea
Only head/face wounds have fixed arsh; all others require just assessment.

This passage from Al-Hidayah establishes a fundamental distinction in diyat law: wounds on the face and head (shijaj) have fixed compensation amounts transmitted by revelation (tawqif), while wounds elsewhere (jirahah) do not. The ruling is tied to the linguistic definition and the rationale of disfigurement (shayn). Without this distinction, one might mistakenly apply fixed arsh to any wound, but the Hanafi school strictly limits it to the head and face.

Guided Path
1
Establish the linguistic boundary
The term shijaj is linguistically limited to wounds on the face and head. Any wound elsewhere is called jirahah. This linguistic reality is the foundation of the legal ruling.
Arabic usage is decisive in defining the scope of the term.
2
Identify the legal consequence
For shijaj, the law prescribes a fixed compensation (arsh muqaddar). For jirahah, no fixed arsh exists; instead, the judge assesses a fair compensation (hukumat al-'adl).
Fixed arsh is only for head/face; otherwise assessment is required.
3
Trace the dalil (tawqif)
The fixed arsh is established by tawqif – revealed texts that specify amounts only for head and face wounds. These texts do not cover other body parts.
Tawqif is the transmitted source; it cannot be extended by analogy.
4
Analyze the rationale (shayn)
The underlying reason for fixed arsh is the disfigurement (shayn) that persists after healing. This is most evident in the head and face because they are normally exposed; other parts are usually covered.
The rationale reinforces the limitation to head/face.
5
Note the Hanafi position on qiyas
Some schools attempt to extend fixed arsh to other wounds by qiyas (analogy), but the Hanafi school rejects this because the textual specification is too strong, and the rationale is not equally present.
Hanafis prioritize textual limitation over general analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم هذه الشجاج تختص بالوجه والرأس لغة وما كان فى غير الوجه والرأس يسمى جراحة والحكم مرتب على الحقيقة في الصحيح حتى لو تحققت في غيرها نحو الساق واليد لا يكون لها ارض مقدر انما تجب حكومة العدل لان التقدير بالتوقيف وهو انما وردفيما تختص بهما ولانه انما ورد الحكم فيها لمعنى الشين الذى يلحقه ببقاء اثر الجراحة والشين يختص بما يظهر منها فى الغالب وهو العضوان هذان لا سواهما
Then these shijaj are specific to the face and head linguistically. And what is in other than the face and head is called jirahah. And the ruling is based on the linguistic reality according to the correct view, such that if they occur in other than these, like the shin or the hand, there is no fixed arsh for them; rather, hukumat al-'adl is obligatory because the determination [of arsh] is by tawqif, and tawqif came only regarding what is specific to them. And because the ruling came for them due to the meaning of disfigurement (shayn) that attaches to the remaining trace of the wound, and disfigurement is specific to what appears of them normally, and these two organs [face and head] are such, not others.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage clarifies the scope of shijaj (head and facial wounds) and distinguishes them from jirahah (other bodily wounds). Linguistically, shijaj only apply to the face and head. Thus, a wound on the shin or hand is not called shajjah but jirahah. The legal ruling follows this linguistic definition: for shijaj, there is a fixed compensation (arsh muqaddar) as transmitted in the sources (tawqif). For jirahah elsewhere, there is no fixed arsh; instead, the judge assesses a fair compensation (hukumat al-'adl) based on the severity and the victim's value. The reason is twofold: (1) the fixed arsh is based on transmitted texts (tawqif), which only mention head/face wounds; (2) the rationale is disfigurement (shayn), which is most evident in exposed areas like the face and head. Other body parts are usually covered, so the disfigurement is less apparent and thus left to assessment.

Key Terms
شجاج (shijaj): Wounds specific to the face and head, for which fixed arsh is prescribed.
جراحة (jirahah): Wounds on other parts of the body, subject to government assessment (hukumat al-'adl).
حكومة العدل (hukumat al-'adl): A just assessment of compensation by a judge, based on the victim's value and the severity of injury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling for a wound on the hand?
A) It has a fixed arsh of the same amount as a head wound.
B) It is subject to hukumat al-'adl (government assessment).
C) There is no compensation for it.
D) It is considered a shajjah.

Key Takeaway
Wounds on the face and head (shijaj) have fixed arsh; all other wounds (jirahah) require judicial assessment.

Are the Jaws Part of the Face? A Hanafi-Maliki Dispute
Resolves a dispute on whether the jaws are included in the face for shijaj rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether the jaws are part of the face for shijaj rulings and explain the reasoning and thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
The jaws are part of the face – connection without separation defines facial boundary for arsh.

This passage addresses a key dispute in diyat law: whether wounds on the jawbones (lahyani) qualify for the fixed compensation (arsh) reserved for facial injuries. Imam Malik argues that the face is only what directly faces an observer, excluding the jaws. The Hanafi school counters that the jaws are integrally connected to the face and partially face the observer, so the same fixed arsh applies. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to identify how definitional reasoning (ta'lil bi al-ism vs. bi al-illa) shapes legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Determine the exact point of disagreement: whether the jaws qualify as part of the face for fixed arsh.
2
State the Maliki position and its linguistic argument
Recall Imam Malik's view: the jaws are not part of the face because 'face' derives from 'muwajahah' (facing), and the jaws do not directly face an observer.
3
State the Hanafi position and its two arguments
The Hanafis include the jaws because they are directly connected to the face without separation, and they also partially face the observer.
4
Define the thamarah al-khilaf
Understand the practical difference: Hanafi applies fixed arsh for jaw wounds; Maliki applies hukumat al-'adl (equitable assessment).
5
Recognize the Hanafi principle
Note that the Hanafi reasoning relies on structural connection and the possibility of facing, not just direct line of sight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واما اللحيان فقد قيل ليسا من الوجه وهو قول مالك حتى لووجد فيهما ما فيه ارش مقدر لا يجب المقدر وهذا الان الوجه مشتق من المواجهة ولا مواجهة للناظر فيهما الا ان عندناهما من الوجه لا تصا لهما به من غير فاصلة وقد يتحقق فيه معنى المواجهة ايضا
As for the two jaws, it has been said that they are not part of the face, and this is the statement of Malik — such that if a wound needing fixed arsh occurs in them, the fixed [arsh] is not obligatory. And this is because the face is derived from facing (muwajahah), and there is no facing for the observer toward them. However, according to us (Hanafis), both are part of the face due to their connection to it without any separation, and the meaning of facing may also be realized in them.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage addresses a difference of opinion regarding whether the two jaw bones (lahyani) are considered part of the face for the purpose of applying fixed arsh for shijaj. Imam Malik holds that they are not part of the face, because the face is linguistically derived from 'muwajahah' (facing), and the jaws do not directly face an observer. Therefore, wounds on the jaws would not qualify for fixed arsh but would fall under hukumat al-'adl. The Hanafi school, however, includes the jaws as part of the face. Their reasoning: The jaws are directly connected to the face without any intervening space, and they are also visible to an observer, especially from certain angles. Hence, wounds on the jaws are subject to the same fixed arsh as other facial wounds.

Key Terms
لحيان (lahyani): The two jaw bones; considered part of the face in Hanafi school for shijaj rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling for a wound on the jaw?
A) It is not subject to fixed arsh because the jaw is not part of the face.
B) It is subject to fixed arsh because the jaw is considered part of the face.
C) It is always subject to qisas.
D) It is subject to hukumat al-'adl only.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school includes the jaws in the face for arsh due to structural continuity, making jaw wounds subject to fixed compensation.

Shijaj: Fixed Arsh Only for Face/Head Wounds
Defines shijaj and rules that wounds elsewhere have hukumat 'adl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that fixed arsh is wajib only for wounds on the face and head, and distinguish when hukumat 'adl applies.

Core Idea
Fixed arsh is wajib only for face/head wounds; all other wounds require hukumat 'adl.

This lesson clarifies a key distinction in Hanafi diyat law: fixed compensation (arsh) is not universal. The Prophet's ﷺ guidance (tawqif) established fixed amounts only for facial and cranial wounds. Understanding this limitation prevents incorrect extension of fixed penalties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine whether a wound is on the face/head or elsewhere, and state whether fixed arsh or hukumat 'adl is wajib.
The compensation itself is the legal consequence.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites 'لان التقدير بالتوقيف' – fixed amounts rely on revelation (tawqif), not analogy.
Tawqif is a key Hanafi principle for penal fixed amounts.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Tawqif only came for wounds on the face and head; therefore, fixed arsh is limited to those locations.
The causation is based on the presence of tawqif.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Abu al-Layth held a broader view; the correct opinion (في الصحيح) restricts fixed arsh to face/head.
The author distinguishes from intra-Hanafi difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الديات

اشرف البدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد - ۱۵
على الحقيقة في الصحيح حتى لو تحققت في غيرها نحو الساق واليد لا يكون لها ارش مقدر وانما تجب حكومة العدل لان التقدير بالتوقيف وهو انما ورد فيما يختص بهما ولانه انما ورد الحكم فيها لمعنى الشين الذي يلحقه ببقاء اثر الجراحة والشين يختص بما يظهر منها في الغالب وهو العضوان هذان لا سواهما

ترجمہ پھر یہ شجاج چہرہ اور سر کے ساتھ مختص ہیں باعتبار لغت کے اور جو زخم سر اور چہرہ کے غیر میں ہو اور اس کو جراحت کہتے ہیں اور حکم مرتب ہوتا ہے حقیقت لغوی پر صحیح قول کے مطابق یہاں تک کہ اگر زخم ان دونوں کے غیر میں ہو جیسے پنڈلی اور ہاتھ تو اس زخم کے لئے مقررہ دیت نہیں ہوگی ، اور حکومت عدل واجب ہوگی اس لئے کہ ارش کی تقدیر توقیف کی وجہ سے ہوتی ہے اور توقیف وارد ہوئی ہے اس زخم کے سلسلہ میں جو ان دونوں ( چہرہ اور سر ) کے ساتھ مخصوص ہو اور اس لئے کہ ان زخموں میں حکم وارد ہوا ہے، اس عیب کے معنی کی وجہ سے جو اس کو لاحق ہوتا ہے جراحت کا اثر باقی رہنے کی وجہ سے اور عیب مخصوص ہے زخموں میں سے ایسے زخم کے ساتھ جو اکثر اوقات کھلا رہتا ہے اور ایسے یہی دو عضو ہیں ۔ تشریح شجاج کی حقیقت لغوی یہ ہے کہ یہ وہ زخم کہلاتا ہے جو صرف چہرہ اور سر میں ہو لہذا اگر زخم سر اور چہرہ کے علاوہ کسی اور جگہ ہو تو وہاں ارش کا یہ حکم جاری نہ ہو گا جو شجاج میں بیان کیا گیا ہے کیونکہ لفظ کی جو حقیقت لغوی ہوتی ہے اس کے اعتبار سے حکم شرعی ثابت ہوتا ہے تو جب حقیقت لغوی مخصوص زخم ہے تو حکم شرعی دوسرے زخم کی وجہ سے ثابت نہ ہوگا۔ ابواللیث کے قول سے احتراز کی وجہ سے مصنف نے فی الصحیح کی قید لگادی ہے لہذا اگر ہاتھ یا پنڈلی وغیرہ میں زخم ہو جائے تو وہاں یہ دیت واجب نہ ہوگی بلکہ اس میں حکومت عدل ہے۔ اس لئے کہ ارش کی تقدیر یقین قیاسی تک بندیوں سے بغیر توقیف و سماع کے نہیں ہو سکتی اور توقیف صرف شجاج میں ہے اور ہاتھ اور پنڈلی کا زخم شجاج میں داخل نہیں ہے۔ پھر دوسری وجہ یہ ہے کہ شجاج میں ارش کا حکم جو وارد ہوا ہے اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ اگر چہ زخم ٹھیک ہو جائے لیکن اس کا نشان جو باقی رہے گا اس کی وجہ سے چہرہ خراب ہو جائے گا اس لئے ارش مقدر واجب ہوتا ہے اور عیب کا ظہور فقط انہیں اعضاء میں ہو گا جو عموما کھلے رہتے ہیں اور ایسے اعضاء چہرہ اور سر ہی ہیں خصوصا چہرہ تو کھلا ہی رہتا ہے۔
Then these shijaj are specific to the face and head linguistically. In the correct opinion, even if they occur elsewhere like the shin or hand, there is no fixed arsh; rather hukumat 'adl is obligatory because the estimation is by tawqif, and tawqif only came for what is specific to them (face and head), and because the ruling came due to the meaning of disfigurement that remains after the wound, and disfigurement is specific to these two limbs which are usually exposed.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the book of diyat. The author states that shijaj (specific wounds of the head and face) are defined by their location (face and head) according to linguistic reality. If such a wound occurs on other body parts like the shin or hand, there is no fixed arsh (blood money) but rather hukumat 'adl (equitable assessment). The proof is that fixed amounts (taqdir) are based on tawqif (revelatory text), and tawqif only exists for wounds on the face and head. Moreover, the purpose of arsh is compensation for permanent disfigurement, which is most noticeable on the face and head. The author adds the qualification 'in the correct opinion' to avoid the view of Abu al-Layth, who apparently considered a broader scope. Two reasons are given: first, fixed arsh cannot be determined by analogical reasoning (qiyas); second, the ruling for shijaj is based on the aspect of 'aib (defect) that remains after healing, which is most manifest on face and head.

Key Terms
شجاج (shijaj): Plural of shajjah, referring to specific wounds of the head and face.
ارش (arsh): Fixed compensation for wounds, determined by law.
حكومة عدل (hukumat 'adl): Equitable assessment of damages by a judge when no fixed arsh applies.
توقيف (tawqif): A ruling based on revelatory text (Quran or Sunnah) rather than analogy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, fixed arsh (مقدر) is wajib for wounds on which body parts?
A) Any part of the body
B) Only the face and head
C) Only the abdomen
D) Only the limbs

Key Takeaway
Fixed arsh is wajib only for wounds on the face and head; all other wounds require hukumat 'adl.

Jawbones and the Face: Hanafi vs. Maliki on Shijaj
Presents the dispute on whether jawbones are part of the face for shijaj rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi and Maliki positions on whether the jawbones are part of the face, identify their respective proofs, and apply the ruling to determine arsh for wounds on the jawbones.

Core Idea
Hanafis include jawbones in the face due to contiguity; Malikis exclude them based on etymology.

This lesson examines a specific dispute in diyat (blood money) law: whether wounds on the jawbones (lahyan) qualify for fixed arsh under the shijaj (head wounds) categories. Imam Malik argued that the jawbones are not part of the face because the word 'wajh' derives from 'muwajahah' (facing), and jawbones do not directly face an observer. The Hanafi school counters that the jawbones are attached to the face without a gap and also involve some degree of facing, thus they are part of the face. This divergence has practical consequences for calculating diyat.

Guided Path
1
State the legal issue
Identify the contested matter: Are the two jawbones (lahyan) considered part of the face (wajh) for the purpose of shijaj rulings? This affects whether a wound on them incurs fixed arsh or requires hukumat 'adl.
2
Analyze the Maliki proof
Examine Imam Malik's evidence: the term 'wajh' is derived from 'muwajahah' (facing), and the jawbones do not face the observer directly. Therefore, they are not part of the face, and wounds on them do not qualify for fixed arsh.
The linguistic derivation is central to this argument.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
Study the Hanafi response: the jawbones are contiguous to the face without any separation, and they also participate in the meaning of muwajahah (the observer sees them). Hence, they are part of the face, and wounds on them incur fixed arsh.
The Hanafi position relies on physical contiguity and a broader interpretation of 'facing'.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Understand the practical consequence: In the Hanafi school, a wound on the jawbone that fits a shijaj category (e.g., a wound that exposes bone) mandates a fixed arsh (e.g., 5 camels for a mudiḥah). In the Maliki school, such a wound would not have a fixed arsh and would be subject to hukumat 'adl (arbitrated compensation) determined by a judge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لحیان ( جبڑے ) شجاج میں داخل ہیں یا نہیں
واما اللحيان فقد قيل ليسا من الوجه وهو قول مالك حتى لووجد فيهما ما فيه ارش مقدر لا يجب المقدر وهذا الان الوجه مشتق من المواجهة ولا مواجهة للناظر فيهما الا ان عندناهما من الوجه لا تصا لهما به من غير فاصلة وقد يتحقق فيه معنى المواجهة ايضا

ترجمہ اور بہر حال دونوں جبڑے پس کہا گیا ہے کہ وہ دونوں چہرہ سے نہیں ہیں اور یہی مالک کا قول ہے یہاں تک کہ اگر ان دونوں میں وہ زخم پایا جائے جس میں ارش ہے تو وہ مقدار ارش واجب نہ ہوگا اور یہ اس لئے کہ وجہ مواجہت سے مشتق ہے اور ناظر کے لئے ان دونوں میں مواجبت نہیں ہے مگر ہمارے نزدیک یہ دونوں چہرہ میں سے ہیں ان دونوں کے متصل ہونے کی وجہ سے چہرہ بغیر فاصلہ کے اور اس میں مواجہت کے معنی بھی متحقق ہیں۔

تشریح دونوں جبڑوں کے بارے میں اختلاف ہے کہ یہ دونوں چہرہ میں ہیں یا نہیں۔ امام مالک نے فرمایا کہ یہ دونوں چہرہ میں داخل نہیں ہیں اس وجہ سے وضو میں ان دونوں کا دھونا فرض نہیں ہے نیز انہوں نے کہا ہے کہ وجہ" مواجہت سے ماخوذ ہے چہرہ کو وجہ" اس لئے کہتے ہیں کہ یہ حصہ دیکھنے والے کے سامنے رہتا ہے لیکن دونوں جبڑے سامنے نہیں رہتے لہذا معلوم ہوا کہ یہ دونوں چہرہ میں داخل نہیں ہیں ۔ ہم نے کہا کہ یہ دونوں بھی مواجہت میں واقع ہوتے ہیں نیز دونوں کا بغیر کسی فاصلہ کے چہرہ سے اتصال ہے لہذا یہ دونوں چہرہ میں داخل ہیں تو جو زخم ان دونوں کا ہوگا اس کی وہی ارش ہو گی جو شجاج میں مذکور ہوا ہے۔

تنبیه ذقن بالاجماع چہرہ میں داخل ہے ٹھوڑی کے نیچے وہ ہڈی جس پر داڑھی اگتی ہے اس کو لحیہ کہتے ہیں۔ تنبیہ - جس طرح ذقن سے مواجہت کا ثبوت ہوتا ہے اس طرح دونوں جبڑوں سے بھی ہوتا ہے ( کما ہو الظاہر ) تو یہ دونوں ذقن کے حکم میں ہو کر چہرہ میں داخل ہو گئے۔ تنبیہ - قیاس کا تقاضہ یہ تھا کہ یہ دونوں چہرہ میں داخل ہیں تو وضو میں ان کا دھونا فرض ہوتا مگر ہم نے اجتماع کی وجہ سے غسل کو واجب نہیں کہا، اور اجماع یہاں شجاج کے بارے میں نہیں ہے تو یہاں ہم نے قیاس پر عمل کرتے ہوئے حقیقت پر عمل کیا۔

تنبیہ - یہاں مصنف نے کہا ہے کہ وجہ مواجہت سے مشتق ہے حالانکہ ثلاثی مزید سے مشتق نہیں ہوتا ؟ جواب معترض غلطی پر ہے اس لئے کہ اتفاق کبیر میں دو لفظوں میں الفاظ و معنی کا تناسب کافی ہوتا ہے اس لئے صاحب کشاف نے کہا ہے کہ یم یتیم سے اور برج تبرج سے مشتق ہے، تنبیه - دونوں جبڑوں کا حکم بیان کرنے میں عبارت کچھ منتشری ہے جس سے خواہ مخواہ ذہن الجھتا ہے صاف مسئلہ یہ ہے جو میں نے عرض کیا ہے۔

تنبیہ - اس مسئلے کو بیان کرنے میں سب سے زیادہ صاف عبارت زیلعی کی ہے ورنہ عبارات میں الجھاؤ ہے۔
As for the two jawbones, it has been said they are not part of the face, which is Malik's opinion. Even if a wound occurs on them that would otherwise have a fixed arsh, the fixed arsh is not wajib. This is because 'wajh' (face) is derived from 'muwajahah' (facing), and there is no facing for the observer in them. However, according to us (Hanafis), they are part of the face due to their attachment to the face without separation, and the meaning of muwajahah also applies to them.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam Malik regarding whether the jawbones (lahyan) are considered part of the face (wajh) for the purpose of shijaj rulings. Imam Malik argues that they are not part of the face because the word 'wajh' is derived from 'muwajahah' (facing), and the jawbones do not face the observer directly; they are not in front. Thus, wounds on the jawbones do not qualify for fixed arsh under shijaj. The Hanafi response (عندنا) is that the jawbones are part of the face because they are attached to the face without any gap, and they do face the observer to some extent. Therefore, wounds on the jawbones do incur the fixed arh of shijaj. The commentary includes several notes: the chin (dhaqn) is unanimously part of the face; the jawbones are analogous to the chin in terms of being visible; although analogy (qiyas) would require washing them in wudu, there is consensus (ijma') that they are not washed in wudu, but that consensus does not extend to shijaj, so here we follow qiyas and linguistic reality. There is also a linguistic note refuting a criticism about derivation from a verb form.

Key Terms
لحيان (lahyan): The two jawbones (mandibles).
وجه (wajh): Face, derived from مواجهة (facing).
مواجهة (muwajahah): Facing directly; the property of being in front of an observer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The word 'wajh' is derived from muwajahah and jawbones do not face the observer
C) There is no proof from hadith
D) The blood money for jawbones is specified in the Quran

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the jawbones are part of the face, so wounds on them incur fixed arsh under shijaj, unlike the Maliki school.

Ja'ifah: Inclusion in Shijaj and Its Definition
Defines ja'ifah and demonstrates its inclusion in shijaj.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define ja'ifah, explain its relationship to shijaj using 'umum wa khusus mutlaq', and state that it incurs fixed arsh as a subtype of shijaj.

Core Idea
A penetrating wound (ja'ifah) is a specific type of head wound (shijaj) — inclusion, not separation.

In the chapter on diyat for head wounds (shijaj), the scholars also discuss ja'ifah, a wound that penetrates the body cavity. Why is it mentioned here? This passage clarifies that ja'ifah is not a separate category but falls under shijaj. Understanding this inclusion is essential for applying the correct fixed compensation (arsh).

Guided Path
1
Identify the wound types
Recognize that shajjah refers to head wounds, while ja'ifah is a wound that penetrates the body cavity (jowf) — either head or abdomen.
2
Understand the definition of ja'ifah
Ja'ifah is specifically a wound that reaches the cavity. This is the key condition that distinguishes it from other wounds.
3
Grasp the usul relationship
The relationship between shajjah and ja'ifah is 'umum wa khusus mutlaq' — general and specific absolute. Every ja'ifah is a shajjah, but not every shajjah is a ja'ifah.
This means ja'ifah is entirely included under shijaj.
4
Apply the implication
Because of this inclusion, the rulings for shijaj (fixed arsh) apply to ja'ifah. The specific arsh category depends on which shajjah subtype the ja'ifah corresponds to.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا
جائفة تختص بالجوف جوف الرأس او جوف البطن

ترجمہ اور مشائخ نے کہا ہے کہ جائفہ جوف کے ساتھ مخصوص ہے سر کا جوف ہو یا پیٹ کا جوف ہو۔ تشریح یہاں ایک سوال وارد ہوتا تھا کہ ذکر چل رہا تھا شجاج کا آپ نے اس میں گھسیڑ دیا ہے جائفہ کو ، تو مصنف جواب دیتے ہیں کہ جائفہ اس زخم کو کہتے ہیں جو جوف تک پہنچ جائے ، خواہ وہ جوف سر کا ہو یا پیٹ کا، لہذا معلوم ہوا کہ جائفہ بھی شجاج میں داخل ہے کیونکہ شجہ اور جائفہ میں عموم خصوص مطلق کی نسبت ہے۔
They said: Ja'ifah is specific to the cavity (jowf), whether the cavity of the head or the cavity of the stomach. The scholars said that ja'ifah is specific to the cavity. Explanation: A question arises: the discussion was about shijaj, why was ja'ifah inserted? The author answers: ja'ifah is a wound that reaches the cavity, whether of the head or abdomen. So it becomes clear that ja'ifah is included in shijaj because the relationship between shajjah and ja'ifah is one of general and specific absolute (umum wa khusus mutlaq).
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is a definition and a ruling concerning the relationship between ja'ifah and shijaj. The scholars state that ja'ifah is a wound that penetrates the cavity (jowf) of the body, whether it is the cranial cavity (head) or the abdominal cavity (stomach). The commentator addresses a question: the discussion was about shijaj (head/face wounds), so why is ja'ifah mentioned here? The answer is that ja'ifah is included under the category of shijaj because the relationship between a shajjah (a head wound) and ja'ifah (a penetrating wound) is 'umum wa khusus mutlaq' (general and specific absolute), meaning that every ja'ifah is a shajjah (since it is a wound), but not every shajjah is a ja'ifah. Thus, ja'ifah is a specific subcategory of shijaj. This clarifies that the rules for shijaj regarding fixed arsh apply to ja'ifah as well, but with specific conditions.

Key Terms
جائفة (ja'ifah): A wound that penetrates the body cavity (jowf), whether head or abdomen.
جوف (jowf): The internal cavity of the body, specifically the head cavity (cranium) or abdominal cavity.
عموم خصوص مطلق (umum wa khusus mutlaq): General and specific absolute: a relationship where the specific is entirely included within the general.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They are the same
B) They have a general-specific absolute relationship (عموم وخصوص مطلق)
C) They are completely different
D) Ja'ifah is a type of shajjah but only for the head

Key Takeaway
Ja'ifah is a specific subtype of shijaj, so the rules for shijaj (including fixed arsh) apply to it.

The Two Jaws: Part of the Face for Diyat, Not Wudu
Dispute over inclusion of jaws in the face for diyat and wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the two jaws being part of the face and distinguish its application for diyat versus wudu, citing the role of ijma' and qiyas.

Core Idea
Jaws are part of the face for diyat but excluded from wudu due to ijma' overriding qiyas.

Is the jaw part of the face? The answer depends on the ruling: for diyat, yes; for wudu, no. This lesson explores the Hanafi-Maliki dispute over the definition of 'face' and how consensus (ijma') overrides qiyas in the case of wudu. We will follow the commentary on Al-Hidayah to understand the reasoning behind each position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and opposing views
Clarify that the core question is whether the two jaws are part of the face. Note Imam Malik's view (excluded) and the Hanafi view (included).
The dispute affects diyat (blood money) and wudu rulings.
2
Analyze the Hanafi linguistic argument
Understand the Hanafi claim that 'wajh' (face) is derived from 'muwajahat' (facing). Both jaws face the onlooker and are directly connected without gap, so they qualify as face.
The passage defends the derivation against grammatical objections.
3
Contrast the Maliki linguistic argument
Imam Malik also uses 'muwajahat' but restricts it to what is directly visible; jaws are not directly facing, so they are not face.
Both schools use the same root, differing on its application.
4
Examine the role of ijma' and qiyas
Note that qiyas would require washing the jaws in wudu, but there is ijma' (consensus) that they are not washed. This ijma' overrides qiyas for wudu. For diyat, no such ijma' exists, so qiyas applies and jaws are included.
This is the key distinction: ijma' only pertains to wudu, not to diyat.
5
Summarize the practical outcome
State the Hanafi mufta bihi: jaws are part of the face for diyat (same 'arsh' as shijaj), but not washed in wudu due to consensus.
The passage recommends Zayla'i's clear expression.

دونوں میں مواجبت نہیں ہے مگر ہمارے نزدیک یہ دونوں چہرہ میں سے ہیں ان دونوں کے متصل ہونے کی وجہ سے چہرہ بغیر فاصلہ کے اور اس میں مواجہت کے معنی بھی متحقق ہیں۔
تشریح دونوں جبڑوں کے بارے میں اختلاف ہے کہ یہ دونوں چہرہ میں ہیں یا نہیں۔ امام مالک نے فرمایا کہ یہ دونوں چہرہ میں داخل نہیں ہیں اس وجہ سے وضو میں ان دونوں کا دھونا فرض نہیں ہے نیز انہوں نے کہا ہے کہ وجہ " مواجہت سے ماخوذ ہے چہرہ کو وجہ" اس لئے کہتے ہیں کہ یہ حصہ دیکھنے والے کے سامنے رہتا ہے لیکن دونوں جبڑے سامنے نہیں رہتے لہذا معلوم ہوا کہ یہ دونوں چہرہ میں داخل نہیں ہیں ۔ ہم نے کہا کہ یہ دونوں بھی مواجہت میں واقع ہوتے ہیں نیز دونوں کا بغیر کسی فاصلہ کے چہرہ سے اتصال ہے لہذا یہ دونوں چہرہ میں داخل ہیں تو جوز ثم ان دونوں کا ہوگا اس کی وہی ارش ہو گی جو شجات میں مذکور ہوا ہے۔
تنبیه ذقن بالا جماع چیرہ میں داخل ہے ٹھوڑی کے نیچے وہ ہڈی جس پر داڑھی اگتی ہے اس کو لحیہ کہتے ہیں۔ تنبیہ - جس طرح ذقن سے مواجہت کا ثبوت ہوتا ہے اس طرح دونوں جبڑوں سے بھی ہوتا ہے ( کما ہو الظاہر ) تو یہ دونوں ذقن کے حکم میں ہو کر چہرہ میں داخل ہو گئے۔ تنبیہ - قیاس کا تقاضہ یہ تھا کہ یہ دونوں چہرہ میں داخل ہیں تو وضو میں ان کا دھونا فرض ہوتا مگر ہم نے اجتماع کی وجہ سے غسل کو واجب نہیں کہا، اور اجماع یہاں شجات کے بارے میں نہیں ہے تو یہاں ہم نے قیاس پر عمل کرتے ہوئے حقیقت پر عمل کیا۔
تنبیہ - یہاں مصنف نے کہا ہے کہ وجہ مواجہت سے مشتق ہے حالانکہ ثلاثی مزید سے مشتق نہیں ہوتا ؟ جواب معترض غلطی پر ہے اس لئے کہ اتفاق کبیر میں دو لفظوں میں الفاظ و معنی کا تناسب کافی ہوتا ہے اس لئے صاحب کشاف نے کہا ہے کہ یم یتیم سے اور برج تبرج سے مشتق ہے، تنبیه - دونوں جبڑوں کا حکم بیان کرنے میں عبارت کچھ منتشری ہے جس سے خواہ مخواہ ذہن الجھتا ہے صاف مسئلہ یہ ہے جو میں نے عرض کیا ہے۔
تنبیہ - اس مسئلے کو بیان کرنے میں سب سے زیادہ صاف عبارت زیلعی کی ہے ورنہ عبارات میں الجھاؤ ہے۔
There is no facing (muwajahat) in them both, but according to us, both are part of the face due to their contiguity with the face without any gap, and the meaning of facing is also realized in them. Explanation: There is disagreement about the two jaws whether they are part of the face or not. Imam Malik said they are not included in the face, therefore washing them in wudu is not obligatory. He also said that 'wajh' (face) is derived from 'muwajahat' (facing), so the part that is in front of the onlooker is called face, but the jaws are not in front, so they are not part of the face. We said both are also included in facing, and they are connected to the face without any gap, so they are part of the face. Therefore, if there is an injury to them, the same 'arsh' compensation applies as mentioned in the chapter on shijaj. Note: The chin is included in the face by consensus. The bone below the chin on which the beard grows is called 'lahyah'. Note: Just as the chin proves facing, similarly both jaws prove it (as is apparent), so they come under the rule of chin and are included in the face. Note: Qiyas required that washing them in wudu would be obligatory, but due to consensus we did not make washing them obligatory, and consensus here is not about shijaj, so here we acted on qiyas and followed reality. Note: The author said 'wajh' is derived from 'muwajahat' although a triliteral root with extra letters is not derived? The answer is that the objector is mistaken because in 'ittifaq kabir', proportionality of words and meanings between two words is sufficient; that is why the author of al-Kashshaf said 'yamm' is derived from 'yatim' and 'burj' from 'tabarruj'. Note: The expression regarding the ruling of the two jaws is somewhat scattered, causing confusion; the clear issue is what I have stated. Note: The clearest expression on this issue is from Zayla'i; other expressions have confusion.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage deals with a dispute between the Hanafis and Imam Malik regarding whether the two jaws (al-jabreyn) are part of the face (al-wajh) for the purposes of diyat and wudu. Imam Malik argues that the face is defined by what is directly facing the onlooker, and since the jaws are not in that position, they are not part of the face. The Hanafis respond that the jaws do face the onlooker and are directly connected to the face without any gap, so they are included. The passage also clarifies that the chin is included by consensus, and the jaws follow the same rule. A note mentions that based on qiyas, washing the jaws in wudu would be obligatory, but due to consensus (ijma') they are not washed, indicating that the consensus here overrides qiyas. The author also addresses a linguistic issue about the derivation of 'wajh' from 'muwajahat', defending the derivation by citing examples from al-Kashshaf. Finally, the author recommends Zayla'i's clearer expression on this matter.

Key Terms
مواجہت (muwajahat): The quality of being directly in front of an onlooker; facing.
ذقن (dhaqan): The chin; specifically the part of the face below the mouth.
لحیہ (lahyah): The bone beneath the chin on which the beard grows.
رش (arsh): Fixed compensation for specific bodily injuries, as opposed to the full diyah for homicide.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why are the two jaws not washed in wudu even though they are considered part of the face?
A) Because the jaw area is unclean.
B) Because Imam Malik's opinion is followed for wudu.
C) Because consensus (ijma') overrides qiyas, and there is ijma' that they are not washed.
D) Because the jaws are not actually part of the face according to the Hanafi school.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school includes the two jaws in the face for diyat based on contiguity and muwajahat, but excludes them from wudu due to ijma' overriding qiyas.

The Relationship between Ja'ifah and Shijaj: General and Specific
Defining ja'ifah and its generality relative to shijaj.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define ja'ifah, differentiate it from shijaj in scope, and apply the ruling of one-third diyat to appropriate wounds.

Core Idea
Ja'ifah is any wound penetrating a body cavity, more general than shijaj, and shares the same diyat as ammah.

In the chapter on shijaj (head wounds), the author unexpectedly introduces ja'ifah. This passage clarifies why: ja'ifah is a broader category that includes some head wounds like ammah. Understanding their relationship is key to correctly classifying wounds under diyat.

Guided Path
1
Identify Definitions
Read the definitions of ja'ifah (wound reaching a cavity) and shijaj (head wounds). Note the specific scope of each.
Ja'ifah can occur in head, chest, abdomen, back, flanks. Shijaj only on the head.
2
Analyse the Relationship
Determine the logical relationship: ja'ifah is general (aamm) and shijaj is specific (khaass) because every shijaj is a wound on the head, but not every ja'ifah is a head wound.
This is 'umum wa khusus mutlaq.
3
Locate the Connection
Understand why ja'ifah is mentioned in the shijaj discussion: ammah (a head wound reaching the brain cavity) is both a shijaj and a ja'ifah, and both share the same diyat ruling.
The common factor is the cavity penetration.
4
Apply the Ruling
Recall that the diyat for ja'ifah and ammah is one-third of full diyat. Apply this to a wound that penetrates a cavity, e.g., a chest wound.
Areas without cavities (below navel, above chin) do not qualify.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا جا ئفہ اور شجہ میں نسبت ائفة تختص بــالـــجــوف جـــوف الـرأس او جوف البطن ترجمہ اور مشائخ نے کہا ہے کہ جائفہ جوف کے ساتھ مخصوص ہے سر کا جوف ہو یا پیٹ کا جوف ہو۔ تشریح یہاں ایک سوال وارد ہوتا تھا کہ ذکر چل رہا تھا شجاج کا آپ نے اس میں گھسیڑ دیا ہے جا ئفہ کو ، تو مصنف جواب دیتے ہیں کہ جا ئفہ اس زخم کو کہتے ہیں جو جوف تک پہنچ جائے ، خواہ وہ جوف سر کا ہو یا پیٹ کا، لہذا معلوم ہوا کہ جا کفہ بھی شجاج میں داخل ہے کیونکہ شجہ اور جا کفہ میں عموم خصوص مطلق کی نسبت ہے۔ جائفہ عام اور شجہ خاص ہے یہ مصنف کے کلام کا حاصل ہے۔ تنبیہ جا کفہ ناف سے نیچے اور ذقن سے اوپر نہیں ہوتا کیونکہ جوف نہیں ہے کیونکہ جوف کے مقامات یہ ہیں، سینہ، کر، پیٹ ، دونوں پہلو، لہذا شجات عشرہ میں جائفہ داخل نہیں ہے۔ تنبیہ تو پھر یہاں متن میں جائفہ کا ذکر کیوں آیا جب کہ یہاں شجاج کا ذکر ہورہا ہے؟ آمہ شجاج میں داخل ہے ( کمامر ) اور آمہ اور جائفہ کا حکم ایک ہے، یعنی تہائی دیت اس لئے آمہ کی مناسبت سے جا ئفہ کا ذکر ہو گیا۔
They said: The relationship between ja'ifah and shijaj: Ja'ifah is specific to the cavity (jawf) – the cavity of the head or the cavity of the abdomen. Translation and the mashayikh said that ja'ifah is specific to the cavity, whether the cavity of the head or the cavity of the abdomen. Explanation: Here a question arises: The discussion was about shijaj (head wounds), but the author has inserted ja'ifah. So the author answers that ja'ifah is a wound that reaches the cavity, whether the cavity of the head or the abdomen. Therefore, it becomes known that ja'ifah is also included in shijaj because the relationship between shijaj and ja'ifah is that of general and specific (al-umum wa al-khusus al-mutlaq). Ja'ifah is general and shijaj is specific – this is the gist of the author's statement. Note: Ja'ifah does not occur below the navel or above the chin because there is no cavity there; the cavities are the chest, back, abdomen, and both flanks. Therefore, ja'ifah is not included among the ten shijaj. Note: Then why is ja'ifah mentioned here in the text when the discussion is about shijaj? Because ammah (a type of head wound) is included in shijaj (as deemed reliable), and the ruling of ammah and ja'ifah is the same, i.e., one-third of the diyat. Therefore, ja'ifah was mentioned due to its similarity to ammah.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage defines the relationship between ja'ifah (a wound penetrating a cavity) and shijaj (head wounds). The scholars state that ja'ifah is specific to any cavity (head or abdomen), so it is more general than shijaj, which is limited to the head. Thus, ja'ifah includes wounds like ammah, which are head wounds reaching the brain cavity. The note clarifies that ja'ifah cannot occur in areas without a cavity (like below navel or above chin). The reason for mentioning ja'ifah in the chapter on shijaj is that ammah, a type of shijaj, has the same ruling: both require one-third of the diyat. This is a definitional and classificatory passage essential for understanding the categories of wounds in diyat law.

Key Terms
جائفہ (ja'ifah): A wound that penetrates into a body cavity (head, chest, abdomen, back, flanks).
شجہ (shijjah): A wound on the head; specifically one of the ten types of head wounds classified in diyat.
آمہ (ammah): A head wound reaching the brain cavity (one of the shijaj).

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because it has the same ruling as ammah, which is a shijaj.
C) Because it occurs only on the head.
D) Because the author made a mistake.

Key Takeaway
Ja'ifah is a wound penetrating any body cavity and is more general than shijaj; its diyat is one-third, shared with ammah.

Hukumat al-Adl: Two Hanafi Methods
Explaining two Hanafi methods of hukumat al-adl.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state and apply the two Hanafi methods of hukumat al-adl to determine diyat for unspecified wounds.

Core Idea
Hukumat al-adl compensates unscripted wounds proportionally — either by market loss or by comparison to mawdihah.

When a wound does not fall into the ten shijaj categories, the diyat is not fixed in the texts. The Hanafi school developed the principle of hukumat al-adl — just valuation — to determine fair compensation. Two prominent methods emerged, attributed to Imam al-Tahawi and Imam al-Karkhi. This passage presents both methods and allows you to understand their application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal gap
Recognize that wounds without a prescribed diyat in the texts require a systematic valuation method.
2
Understand Tahawi's method
Observe that Tahawi uses the decrease in a slave's market value as a ratio to determine the fraction of full diyah owed. The ratio of value loss equals the ratio of diyah.
The slave is an illustrative analogy; the method applies to free persons by estimating loss in value (e.g., earning capacity).
3
Understand Karkhi's method
Observe that Karkhi compares the wound to the standard mawdihah wound (which has a fixed diyat of half of one-tenth of full diyah). He measures how much smaller the current wound is relative to mawdihah and applies that proportion to the mawdihah's diyat.
4
Apply both methods to a scenario
Use the example from the passage: for a decrease of 1/20 in value, Tahawi gives 1/20 of full diyah; Karkhi would compare the wound to mawdihah to find the same fraction.
5
Note the ikhtilaf
Both methods are valid in the Hanafi school; no tarjih is preferred. The mufti may choose based on context.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, not a cross-madhhab dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لفسير حكومة العدل على ما قاله الطحاوى ان يقوم مملوكا بدون هذا الاثر ويقوم وبه هذا الاثر ثم نظر الى تفاوت ما بين القيمتين فان كان نصف عشر القيمة يجب نصف عشر الدية وان كان ربع عشر اور حکومت عدل کی تفسیر اس تفصیل کے مطابق جس کو طحاوی نے بیان کیا ہے یہ ہے کہ مملوک کی بغیر اس اثر کے قیمت لگائی اور اس اثر کے ساتھ قیمت لگائی جائے پھر دونوں قیمتوں کے درمیان تفاوت کو دیکھا جائے پس اگر وہ تفاوت قیمت کے عشر کا نصف ت کا عشر واجب کر دیا جائے اور اگر ربع عشر ہو تو ربع عشر واجب کر دیا جائے۔ موضحہ کے اوپر شجاح کے اندر حکومت عدل کی تفسیر کرتے ہیں اور یہ تفسیر امام طحاوی سے منقول ہے ، فرماتے ہیں کہ زید کے وہ آزاد ہے تو آپ دیکھئے کہ اگر غلام ہوتا تو اس کی بغیر اس زخم کے کیا قیمت ہوتی مثلاً آپ نے اندازہ لگایا کہ اس کی قیمت سو تی اور اس زخم کے ساتھ جب قیمت کا اندازہ پچانوے روپے کے ساتھ ہوا تو دونوں قیمتوں میں پانچ روپے کا فرق آیا اور یہ پانچ کی کل قیمت کے عشر کا نصف ہے یعنی بیسواں ہے تو ایسی صورت میں پوری دیت کا نصف عشر یعنی پانچ اونٹ واجب ہوں گے دوروپے پچاس پیسے کا فرق نکلا ہوتو یہاں دیت کا چالیسواں حصہ واجب ہوگا یعنی ڈھائی اونٹ ۔ وقس على هذا
حکومت عدل میں امام کرخی کا نقطہ نظر برد الى المنصوص عليه. رخی ینظر کم مقدار هذه الشجة من الموضحة فيجب بقدر ذالك من نصف عشر الدية لان ئے اس لئے کہ وہ چیز جس میں نص نہ ہو اس کو منصوص علیہ کی جانب پھیرا جاتا ہے۔ نی نے فرمایا کہ دیکھا جائے اس زخم کی موضحہ کے حساب سے کیا مقدار ہے پس دیت کے نصف عشر میں سے اس کے بقدر عدل کی تفسیر امام کرخی سے منقول ہے فرماتے ہیں کہ جس چیز میں نص نہ ہو تو اس کا حکم معلوم کرنے کا طریقہ یہ ہے کہ طرف پھیر کر اس سے حساب لگا کر اس کی مقدار متعین کرنی چاہئے ۔ ندرانص موجود ہے کہ اس میں نصف العشر واجب ہے، الہذا اب موضحہ کو دیکھو اور اس زخم کو دیکھو کہ موضحہ اس کا زخم کتنا ہلکا
The interpretation of hukumat al-adl according to what al-Tahawi said: A slave is valued without this effect, and valued with this effect, then the difference between the two values is looked at. If it is half of one-tenth of the value, then half of one-tenth of the diyah is obligated; if it is one-quarter of one-tenth, then one-quarter of one-tenth is obligated. The interpretation of hukumat al-adl for wounds above mawdihah within shijaj is narrated from Imam Tahawi. He says: Consider Zaid who is free; see what his value would be without this wound. For example, you estimate his value at one hundred. Then when his value with this wound is estimated at ninety-five, the difference is five. This five is half of one-tenth of the total value, i.e., one-twentieth. In that case, half of one-tenth of the full diyah is obligated, i.e., five camels. If the difference had been two and a half, then one-fortieth of the diyah would be obligated, i.e., two and a half camels. And so on. Regarding the perspective of Imam al-Karkhi on hukumat al-adl: It is referred to the textually prescribed (al-mansus 'alayh). Al-Karkhi says: Look at how much this wound is relative to mawdihah; then that portion of half of one-tenth of the diyah is obligated, because the matter for which there is no text is turned to the textually prescribed. He said that the method to know the ruling of that for which there is no text is to turn it to the text and calculate its extent. Since a text exists that half of one-tenth is obligatory for mawdihah, so now look at mawdihah and this wound to see how much lighter this wound is compared to mawdihah.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage explains the concept of hukumat al-adl (just valuation) for wounds that do not have a specified diyat (like the ten shijaj). Two scholarly views are presented: Imam Tahawi's method and Imam al-Karkhi's method. Tahawi's method uses the analogy of a slave: calculate the difference in market value of the slave with and without the wound, then apply that fraction to the full diyah. For example, if the value decreases by 1/20, then 1/20 of the full diyah is due. Karkhi's method relates the wound to the mawdihah (a standard shijaj that exposes bone), which has a prescribed diyat of half of one-tenth of full diyah (5 camels). He says to measure how much smaller the current wound is compared to mawdihah, and then apply that proportion to that half-tenth. Both methods aim to derive a fair compensation where no specific amount is textually prescribed. The passage is essential for understanding diyat calculation for non-standard injuries.

Key Terms
حکومت عدل (hukumat al-adl): A just valuation method to determine diyat for wounds not covered by explicit textual amounts.
موضحہ (mawdihah): A head wound that exposes the bone; one of the ten shijaj with a prescribed diyat of half of one-tenth of full diyah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Tahawi's method for hukumat al-adl, if a slave's value decreases by 1/20 due to a wound, how much diyat is due?
A) 1/20 of the full diyah.
B) half of one-tenth of the full diyah.
C) 1/10 of the full diyah.
D) 1/40 of the full diyah.

Key Takeaway
Hukumat al-adl is the Hanafi method for compensating unspecified wounds, using either Tahawi's market loss ratio or Karkhi's comparison to mawdihah.

Jaa'ifah and Its Distinction from Shijaj
Clarifying that jaa'ifah is not a shajjah but mentioned due to shared ruling with ammah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of jaa'ifah, explain why it is not a shajjah, and identify the ruling on its diyah.

Core Idea
Jaa'ifah is not a head wound but a cavity wound; its one-third diyah is shared with ammah.

In the chapter on diyat, the author discusses shijaj (head wounds) but also mentions jaa'ifah. This lesson clarifies that jaa'ifah is not a type of shajjah, yet it appears here because its ruling—one-third diyah—matches that of ammah, a specific head wound. Understanding this distinction prevents misclassification of wounds for legal compensation.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish jaa'ifah from shajjah
Jaa'ifah is any wound penetrating into a body cavity (jawf), while shajjah is specific to head/face wounds. Recognize that jaa'ifah is a broader category.
The author states: 'Jaa'ifah is general and shajjah is specific.'
2
Identify the anatomical boundaries of jaa'ifah
Jaa'ifah only occurs in areas that are considered cavities: chest, stomach, and both flanks. It does not occur below the navel or above the chin.
This excludes the lower abdomen and the area above the chin (e.g., neck).
3
Explain why jaa'ifah is not among the ten shijaj
The ten shijaj are defined exclusively for head/face injuries. Since jaa'ifah involves body cavities, it is omitted from that list.
4
Understand the connection to ammah
Ammah is a head wound reaching the brain membrane and is part of the shijaj. Both ammah and jaa'ifah share the same diyah: one-third of full diyah. Therefore, jaa'ifah is mentioned here for comparison.
This explains why the text includes jaa'ifah even though it is not a shajjah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الديات جائفہ عام اور شجہ خاص ہے یہ مصنف کے کلام کا حاصل ہے۔ تنبیہ جائفہ ناف سے نیچے اور ذقن سے اوپر نہیں ہوتا کیونکہ جوف نہیں ہے کیونکہ جوف کے مقامات یہ ہیں، سینہ، کر، پیٹ، دونوں پہلو، لہذا شجاج عشرہ میں جائفہ داخل نہیں ہے۔ تنبیہ تو پھر یہاں متن میں جائفہ کا ذکر کیوں آیا جب کہ یہاں شجاج کا ذکر ہورہا ہے؟ آمہ شجاج میں داخل ہے (کمامر) اور آمہ اور جائفہ کا حکم ایک ہے، یعنی تہائی دیت اس لئے آمہ کی مناسبت سے جائفہ کا ذکر ہو گیا۔
Book of Diyat. Jaa'ifah is general and shajjah is specific; this is the summary of the author's words. Note: Jaa'ifah does not occur below the navel and above the chin because that is not a cavity; the cavity areas are: chest, [؟], stomach, and both flanks. Therefore, jaa'ifah is not included in the ten shijaj. Note: Then why is jaa'ifah mentioned here when shijaj are being discussed? Ammah is included in shijaj (as mentioned) and the ruling of ammah and jaa'ifah is the same, i.e., one-third diyah. So jaa'ifah was mentioned due to its connection with ammah.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement clarifying the legal status of jaa'ifah (a wound that penetrates into a body cavity) in relation to shijaj (head wounds). The author first distinguishes jaa'ifah as a general category and shajjah as a specific type. He then issues a note (tanbeeh) that jaa'ifah does not occur in areas below the navel or above the chin because those are not cavities; the recognized cavities in Hanafi fiqh are the chest, the stomach, and the two flanks. Therefore, jaa'ifah is not among the enumerated ten types of shijaj. However, a second note addresses why jaa'ifah is mentioned in this section: because ammah (a head wound that reaches the brain membrane) is included in shijaj, and both ammah and jaa'ifah carry the same ruling—one-third diyah. Thus, jaa'ifah is mentioned here due to its connection with ammah, not because it is a shajjah itself.

Key Terms
جائفہ (jaa'ifah): A wound that penetrates into a body cavity (jawf), such as the chest, stomach, or flank.
شجہ (shajjah): A wound on the head or face specifically, classified into ten types.
آمہ (ammah): A head wound that reaches the brain membrane (the thinnest covering of the brain).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because ammah, which shares the same ruling as jaa'ifah, is among shijaj.
C) Because the author made a mistake.
D) Because jaa'ifah occurs only on the head.

Key Takeaway
Jaa'ifah is a cavity wound distinct from shijaj, but it is discussed alongside them because its diyah (one-third) matches that of ammah.

Two Methods of Hukumat Adl: Tahawi and Karkhi
Explaining two methods for assessing equitable compensation for non-prescribed injuries

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two methods of hukumat adl as defined by Imam al-Tahawi and Imam al-Karkhi, and apply them to a non-prescribed injury using qiyas.

Core Idea
Hukumat adl: assess non-prescribed wounds by value difference (Tahawi) or analogy to mudihah (Karkhi).

This passage presents two classical Hanafi methods for determining diyah when a wound has no fixed prescribed amount. Imam al-Tahawi uses a market value comparison, while Imam al-Karkhi analogizes to the prescribed mudihah. Understanding both methods is essential for correctly applying qiyas in diyat cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the injury as non-prescribed
Determine that the wound does not have a fixed diyah amount in the Shari'ah (e.g., it is not one of the ten shijaj or other prescribed injuries).
Prescribed injuries have fixed diyah; all others fall under hukumat adl.
2
Apply Tahawi's value-difference method
Find the market value of a hypothetical slave without the injury, then with the injury. The difference as a fraction of the original value becomes the fraction of diyah owed. For example, if the value drops by 1/20, then 1/20 of full diyah (5 camels) is wajib.
This method is analogous to 'arsh (compensation for property damage) applied to the person.
3
Apply Karkhi's analogy-to-mudihah method
Compare the non-prescribed wound to the prescribed wound mudihah (which exposes bone and carries 1/20 of diyah). Estimate what fraction of mudihah's severity this wound represents, then apply that fraction to 1/20 of diyah. For example, if half as severe, then 1/40 of diyah is wajib.
This is a direct qiyas: the non-prescribed wound is the far' and mudihah is the asl.
4
Note the common principle
Both methods are forms of qiyas. The governing maxim is 'ma la nassa fihi yuradu ila al-mansus alayh' (what is not prescribed is referred to the prescribed).
The difference is the asl (original case) used for analogy: in Tahawi it is a slave's value; in Karkhi it is the mudihah wound.
5
Determine the operative ruling
Both methods are valid within the Hanafi school. The judge may choose either based on evidentiary circumstances. The mufta bihi is often the method that yields the most just outcome.
No single method is exclusively relied upon; both are part of the school's toolkit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حکومت عدل کی تفسیر لفسير حكومة العدل على ما قاله الطحاوى ان يقوم مملوكا بدون هذا الاثر ويقوم وبه هذا الاثر ثم نظر الى تفاوت ما بين القيمتين فان كان نصف عشر القيمة يجب نصف عشر الدية وان كان ربع عشر يجب ربع عشر الدية وقس على هذا. حکومت عدل میں امام کرخی کا نقطہ نظر برد الى المنصوص عليه. رخی ینظر کم مقدار هذه الشجة من الموضحة فيجب بقدر ذالك من نصف عشر الدية لان ما ليس بمنصوص عليه يرد الى المنصوص عليه. نی نے فرمایا کہ دیکھا جائے اس زخم کی موضحہ کے حساب سے کیا مقدار ہے پس دیت کے نصف عشر میں سے اس کے بقدر واجب کر دیا جائے. وقس علی ہذا
Interpretation of Hukumat Adl: According to what al-Tahawi said: a slave is appraised without this effect and appraised with this effect, then the difference between the two values is considered. If it is half a tenth of the value, half a tenth of the diyah is wajib; if it is a quarter tenth, a quarter tenth of the diyah is wajib; and so on. In Hukumat Adl, the view of Imam al-Karkhi: refer to the prescribed amount. Al-Karkhi considered: look at how much this wound amounts to relative to mudihah, then according to that amount from half a tenth of the diyah is wajib, because that which is not prescribed is referred to the prescribed. He said: see what quantity this wound is relative to mudihah, then accordingly from half a tenth of the diyah is made wajib. And so on.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage defines hukumat adl (equitable assessment of diyah) for wounds that do not have a prescribed fixed amount. The commentator presents two scholarly views. First, Imam al-Tahawi's method: evaluate a hypothetical slave's market value without the wound and with the wound. The difference in value as a fraction of the total value determines the fraction of diyah. For example, if the difference is half a tenth (i.e., 1/20) of the value, then half a tenth of the full diyah (i.e., 1/20 of 100 camels = 5 camels) is wajib. Second, Imam al-Karkhi's method: compare the wound to the prescribed wound mudihah (which exposes bone and carries one-twentieth of diyah). Find what fraction of mudihah this wound is in terms of severity, then apply that fraction to half a tenth of diyah. For example, if the wound is half the severity of mudihah, then quarter tenth (1/40) of diyah is wajib; if 1/5, then 1/100 of diyah (1 camel), etc. The principle is: non-prescribed injuries are analogized to prescribed ones (ar-radu ila al-mansus alayh). Both methods aim to ensure just compensation using qiyas.

Key Terms
حکومت عدل (hukumat adl): An equitable assessment of diyah for injuries that do not have a fixed prescribed amount, based on the difference in value or analogy to prescribed wounds.
موضحہ (mudihah): A wound that exposes the bone; one of the ten shijaj, with a prescribed diyah of one-twentieth (half a tenth) of full diyah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Tahawi's method, how is the fraction of diyah determined in hukumat adl?
A) By the severity of the wound compared to the size of the limb.
B) By the difference in market value of a slave with and without the wound.
C) By the number of days of treatment.
D) By the opinion of the judge.

Key Takeaway
Hukumat adl for non-prescribed injuries is determined either by value difference (Tahawi) or by analogy to mudihah (Karkhi), both valid methods in Hanafi fiqh.
